<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Decompression sickness</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Disorder caused by dissolved gases in the tissues forming bubbles during reduction of the surrounding pressure</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background:#ccc">Decompression sickness</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Synonyms</th><td>Divers' disease, the bends, aerobullosis, caisson disease</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Decompression_chamber.jpg" title="Photograph of the cramped interior of a cylinder containing two benches"><img alt="Photograph of the cramped interior of a cylinder containing two benches" data-file-height="2601" data-file-width="3753" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Decompression_chamber.jpg/300px-Decompression_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Decompression_chamber.jpg/450px-Decompression_chamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Decompression_chamber.jpg/600px-Decompression_chamber.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Two <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> sailors prepare for training inside a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_chamber" title="Decompression chamber">decompression chamber</a>.</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" title="Specialty (medicine)">Specialty</a></th><td><a href="/wiki/Emergency_medicine" title="Emergency medicine">Emergency medicine</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)" title="Decompression (diving)">Decompression (diving)</a>.</div>
<p><b>Decompression sickness</b> (<b>DCS</b>; also known as <b>divers' disease</b>, <b>the bends</b>, <b>aerobullosis</b>, or <b>caisson disease</b>) describes a condition arising from dissolved gases coming out of solution into bubbles inside the body on depressurisation. DCS most commonly refers to problems arising from underwater <a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)" title="Decompression (diving)">diving decompression</a> (i.e., during ascent), but may be experienced in other depressurisation events such as emerging from a <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(engineering)" title="Caisson (engineering)">caisson</a>, flying in an <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">unpressurised aircraft</a> at altitude, and <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extravehicular activity</a> from spacecraft. DCS and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_gas_embolism" title="Arterial gas embolism">arterial gas embolism</a> are collectively referred to as <a href="/wiki/Decompression_illness" title="Decompression illness">decompression illness</a>.
</p><p>Since bubbles can form in or migrate to any part of the body, DCS can produce many symptoms, and its effects may vary from joint pain and rashes to paralysis and death. Individual susceptibility can vary from day to day, and different individuals under the same conditions may be affected differently or not at all. The classification of types of DCS by its symptoms has evolved since its original description over a hundred years ago.
</p><p>Risk of DCS caused by diving can be managed through proper <a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)" title="Decompression (diving)">decompression procedures</a> and contracting it is now uncommon.  Its potential severity has driven much research to prevent it and divers almost universally use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dive_tables" title="Dive tables">dive tables</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dive_computers" title="Dive computers">dive computers</a> to limit their exposure and to control their ascent speed. If DCS is suspected, it is treated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_oxygen_therapy" title="Hyperbaric oxygen therapy">hyperbaric oxygen therapy</a> in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recompression_chamber" title="Recompression chamber">recompression chamber</a>. If treated early, there is a significantly higher chance of successful recovery.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>DCS is classified by symptoms. The earliest descriptions of DCS used the terms: "bends" for joint or skeletal pain; "chokes" for breathing problems; and "staggers" for neurological problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578-1">[1]</a></sup> In 1960, Golding <i>et al.</i> introduced a simpler classification using the term "Type I ('simple')" for symptoms involving only the <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_system" title="Musculoskeletal system">musculoskeletal system</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lymphatic_system" title="Lymphatic system">lymphatic system</a>, and "Type II ('serious')" for symptoms where other organs (such as the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>) are involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578-1">[1]</a></sup> Type II DCS is considered more serious and usually has worse outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-emed-2">[2]</a></sup> This system, with minor modifications, may still be used today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1908_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1908-3">[3]</a></sup> Following changes to treatment methods, this classification is now much less useful in diagnosis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''579_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''579-4">[4]</a></sup> since neurological symptoms may develop after the initial presentation, and both Type I and Type II DCS have the same initial management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decompression_illness_and_dysbarism">Decompression illness and dysbarism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Decompression illness and dysbarism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Dysbarism" title="Dysbarism">dysbarism</a> encompasses decompression sickness, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_gas_embolism" title="Arterial gas embolism">arterial gas embolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barotrauma" title="Barotrauma">barotrauma</a>, whereas decompression sickness and arterial gas embolism are commonly classified together as <a href="/wiki/Decompression_illness" title="Decompression illness">decompression illness</a> when a precise diagnosis cannot be made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''580_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''580-6">[6]</a></sup>  DCS and arterial gas embolism are treated very similarly because they are both the result of gas bubbles in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-5">[5]</a></sup> The U.S. Navy prescribes identical treatment for Type II DCS and arterial gas embolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNDM2008_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNDM2008-7">[7]</a></sup> Their spectra of symptoms also overlap, although the symptoms from arterial gas embolism are generally more severe because they often arise from an <a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">infarction</a> (blockage of blood supply and tissue death).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While bubbles can form anywhere in the body, DCS is most frequently observed in the shoulders, elbows, knees, and ankles. Joint pain ("the bends") accounts for about 60% to 70% of all altitude DCS cases, with the shoulder being the most common site. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurological" title="Neurological">Neurological</a> symptoms are present in 10% to 15% of DCS cases with <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a> and visual disturbances being the most common symptom. Skin manifestations are present in about 10% to 15% of cases. Pulmonary DCS ("the chokes") is very rare in divers and has been observed much less frequently in aviators since the introduction of oxygen pre-breathing protocols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPowell71_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPowell71-8">[8]</a></sup> The table below shows symptoms for different DCS types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578–584_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''578–584-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption><b>Signs and symptoms of decompression sickness</b>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th scope="col">DCS type
</th>
<th scope="col">Bubble location
</th>
<th scope="col">Signs &amp; symptoms (clinical manifestations)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Musculoskeletal
</th>
<td>Mostly large joints
<p>(elbows, shoulders, hip, wrists, knees, ankles)
</p>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Localized deep pain, ranging from mild to excruciating. Sometimes a dull ache, but rarely a sharp pain.</li>
<li>Active and passive motion of the joint aggravates the pain.</li>
<li>The pain may be reduced by bending the joint to find a more comfortable position.</li>
<li>If caused by altitude, pain can occur immediately or up to many hours later.</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Cutaneous
</th>
<td>Skin
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Itching, usually around the ears, face, neck, arms, and upper torso</li>
<li>Sensation of tiny insects crawling over the skin (<a href="/wiki/Formication" title="Formication">formication</a>)</li>
<li>Mottled or marbled skin usually around the shoulders, upper chest and abdomen, with itching</li>
<li>Swelling of the skin, accompanied by tiny scar-like skin depressions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitting_edema" title="Pitting edema">pitting edema</a>)</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Neurologic
</th>
<td>Brain
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Altered sensation, tingling or numbness (<a href="/wiki/Paresthesia" title="Paresthesia">paresthesia</a>), increased sensitivity (<a href="/wiki/Hyperesthesia" title="Hyperesthesia">hyperesthesia</a>)</li>
<li>Confusion or memory loss (<a href="/wiki/Amnesia" title="Amnesia">amnesia</a>)</li>
<li>Visual abnormalities</li>
<li>Unexplained mood or behaviour changes</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Non-epileptic_seizure" title="Non-epileptic seizure">Seizures</a>, unconsciousness</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Neurologic
</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">Spinal cord</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Ascending weakness or <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a> in the legs</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Urinary_incontinence" title="Urinary incontinence">Urinary incontinence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fecal_incontinence" title="Fecal incontinence">fecal incontinence</a></li>
<li>Girdling (also referred to as girdle, banding, or tightening feeling) around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdominal" title="Abdominal">abdominal</a> region and/or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">chest</a></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Constitutional
</th>
<td>Whole body
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Headache</li>
<li>Unexplained fatigue</li>
<li>Generalised malaise, poorly localised aches</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Audiovestibular
</th>
<td>Inner ear <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12562679_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12562679-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICD_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICD-11">[a]</a></sup>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li>Loss of balance</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dizziness" title="Dizziness">Dizziness</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">vertigo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a></li>
<li>Hearing loss</li></ul>
</td></tr>

</td>

<li><a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">Shortness of breath</a></li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Onset">Onset</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Onset">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although onset of DCS can occur rapidly after a dive, in more than half of all cases symptoms do not begin to appear for at least an hour. In extreme cases, symptoms may occur before the dive has been completed. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy">U.S. Navy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technical_Diving_International" title="Technical Diving International">Technical Diving International</a>, a leading technical diver training organization, have published a table that documents time to onset of first symptoms. The table does not differentiate between types of DCS, or types of symptom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usn520-3.1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-usn520-3.1-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TdiDpm_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-TdiDpm-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

</th>

</th></tr>
<tr>

</th>
<td>42%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">within 3 hours
</th>
<td>60%
</td></tr>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)#Bubble_formation,_growth_and_elimination" title="Decompression (diving)">Decompression (diving) § Bubble formation, growth and elimination</a></div>
<p>DCS is caused by a reduction in <a href="/wiki/Ambient_pressure" title="Ambient pressure">ambient pressure</a> that results in the formation of bubbles of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inert_gases" title="Inert gases">inert gases</a> within tissues of the body. It may happen when leaving a high-pressure environment, ascending from depth, or ascending to altitude.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascent_from_depth">Ascent from depth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ascent from depth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>DCS is best known as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diving_disorder" title="Diving disorder">diving disorder</a> that affects divers having breathed gas that is at a higher pressure than the surface pressure, owing to the pressure of the surrounding water. The risk of DCS increases when diving for extended periods or at greater depth, without ascending gradually and making the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_stops" title="Decompression stops">decompression stops</a> needed to slowly reduce the excess pressure of inert gases dissolved in the body. The specific risk factors are not well understood and some divers may be more susceptible than others under identical conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benton_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benton-16">[15]</a></sup>  DCS has been confirmed in rare cases of <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">breath-holding</a> divers who have made a sequence of many deep dives with short surface intervals; and it may be the cause of the disease called <a href="/wiki/Taravana" title="Taravana">taravana</a> by South Pacific island natives who for centuries have dived by breath-holding for food and <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wong1999_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wong1999-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two principal factors control the risk of a diver suffering DCS:
</p>
<ol><li>the rate and duration of gas absorption under pressure – the deeper or longer the dive the more gas is absorbed into body tissue in higher concentrations than normal (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry%27s_Law" title="Henry's Law">Henry's Law</a>);</li>
<li>the rate and duration of outgassing on depressurization – the faster the ascent and the shorter the interval between dives the less time there is for absorbed gas to be offloaded safely through the lungs, causing these gases to come out of solution and form "micro bubbles" in the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell65–66_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell65–66-18">[17]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Even when the change in pressure causes no immediate symptoms, rapid pressure change can cause permanent <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone</a> injury called <a href="/wiki/Dysbaric_osteonecrosis" title="Dysbaric osteonecrosis">dysbaric osteonecrosis</a> (DON). DON can develop from a single exposure to rapid decompression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohta1974_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ohta1974-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaving_a_high-pressure_environment">Leaving a high-pressure environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Leaving a high-pressure environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caisson_Schematic.svg"><img alt="Schematic of a caisson" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Caisson_Schematic.svg/300px-Caisson_Schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Caisson_Schematic.svg/450px-Caisson_Schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Caisson_Schematic.svg/600px-Caisson_Schematic.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caisson_Schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The principal features of a caisson are the workspace, pressurised by an external air supply, and the access tube with an airlock</div></div></div>
<p>When workers leave a pressurized <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(engineering)" title="Caisson (engineering)">caisson</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mine</a> that has been pressurized to keep water out, they will experience a significant reduction in <a href="/wiki/Ambient_pressure" title="Ambient pressure">ambient pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliott_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott-20">[19]</a></sup> A similar pressure reduction occurs when <a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">astronauts</a> exit a space vehicle to perform a space-walk or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">extra-vehicular activity</a>, where the pressure in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacesuit" title="Spacesuit">spacesuit</a> is lower than the pressure in the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vann2-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original name for DCS was "caisson disease". This term was introduced in the 19th century, when caissons under pressure were used to keep water from flooding large engineering excavations below the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a>, such as bridge supports and tunnels. Workers spending time in high ambient pressure conditions are at risk when they return to the lower pressure outside the caisson if the pressure is not reduced slowly. DCS was a major factor during construction of <a href="/wiki/Eads_Bridge" title="Eads Bridge">Eads Bridge</a>, when 15 workers died from what was then a mysterious illness, and later during construction of the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">Brooklyn Bridge</a>, where it incapacitated the project leader <a href="/wiki/Washington_Roebling" title="Washington Roebling">Washington Roebling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eads_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eads-24">[23]</a></sup> On the other side of the Manhattan island during construction of the <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Hudson_Tubes" title="Downtown Hudson Tubes">Hudson River Tunnel</a> contractor's agent <a href="/wiki/Ernest_William_Moir" title="Ernest William Moir">Ernest William Moir</a> noted in 1889 that workers were dying due to decompression sickness and pioneered the use of an <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a> chamber for treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudson_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascent_to_altitude">Ascent to altitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ascent to altitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most common health risk on ascent to altitude is not decompression sickness but <a href="/wiki/Altitude_sickness" title="Altitude sickness">altitude sickness</a>, or acute mountain sickness (AMS), which has an entirely different and unrelated set of causes and symptoms.  AMS results not from the formation of bubbles from dissolved gasses in the body but from exposure to a low partial pressure of oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Alkalosis" title="Alkalosis">alkalosis</a>. However, passengers in unpressurized aircraft at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_altitude" title="High altitude">high altitude</a> may also be at some risk of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerth_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerth-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Altitude DCS became a problem in the 1930s with the development of high-altitude balloon and aircraft flights but not as great a problem as AMS, which drove the development of <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">pressurized cabins</a>, which coincidentally controlled DCS. Commercial aircraft are now required to maintain the cabin at or below a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_altitude" title="Pressure altitude">pressure altitude</a> of 2,400 m (7,900 ft) even when flying above 12,000 m (39,000 ft).  Symptoms of DCS in healthy individuals are subsequently very rare unless there is a <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization#Loss_of_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">loss of pressurization</a> or the individual has been diving recently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1999_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1999-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RRR1181_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-RRR1181-28">[27]</a></sup> Divers who drive up a mountain or fly shortly after diving are at particular risk even in a pressurized aircraft because the regulatory cabin altitude of 2,400 m (7,900 ft) represents only 73% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_level_pressure" title="Sea level pressure">sea level pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann1_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vann1-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Generally, the higher the altitude the greater the risk of altitude DCS but there is no specific, maximum, safe altitude below which it never occurs.  There are very few symptoms at or below 5,500 m (18,000 ft) unless patients had predisposing medical conditions or had dived recently.  There is a correlation between increased altitudes above 5,500 m (18,000 ft) and the frequency of altitude DCS but there is no direct relationship with the severity of the various types of DCS. A US Air Force study reports that there are few occurrences between 5,500 m (18,000 ft) and 7,500 m (24,600 ft) and 87% of incidents occurred at or above 7,500 m (24,600 ft). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAA2005_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAA2005-30">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_military_parachuting" title="High-altitude military parachuting">High altitude parachutists</a> may reduce the risk of altitude DCS if they flush nitrogen from the body by pre-breathing pure oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14620473_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14620473-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predisposing_factors">Predisposing factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Predisposing factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the occurrence of DCS is not easily predictable, many predisposing factors are known. They may be considered as either environmental or individual.
Decompression sickness and arterial gas embolism in recreational diving are associated with certain demographic, environmental, and dive style factors. A statistical study published in 2005 tested potential risk factors: age, gender, body mass index, smoking, asthma, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, previous decompression illness, years since certification, dives in the last year, number of diving days, number of dives in a repetitive series, last dive depth, nitrox use, and drysuit use. No significant associations with risk of decompression sickness or arterial gas embolism were found for asthma, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, smoking, or body mass index. Increased depth, previous DCI, larger number of consecutive days diving, and being male were associated with higher risk for decompression sickness and arterial gas embolism. Nitrox and drysuit use, greater frequency of diving in the past year, increasing age, and years since certification were associated with lower risk, possibly as indicators of more extensive training and experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeNoble_2005_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeNoble_2005-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">Environmental</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Environmental">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following environmental factors have been shown to increase the risk of DCS:
</p>
<ul><li>the magnitude of the pressure reduction ratio – a large pressure reduction ratio is more likely to cause DCS than a small one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann1_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vann1-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>repetitive exposures – repetitive dives within a short period of time (a few hours) increase the risk of developing DCS. Repetitive ascents to altitudes above 5,500 metres (18,000 ft) within similar short periods increase the risk of developing altitude DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>the rate of ascent – the faster the ascent the greater the risk of developing DCS. The US Navy Dive Manual indicates that ascent rates greater than about 20 m/min (66 ft/min) when diving increase the chance of DCS, while recreational dive tables such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%BChlmann_tables" title="Bühlmann tables">Bühlmann tables</a> require an ascent rate of 10 m/min (33 ft/min) with the last 6 m (20 ft) taking at least one minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell232_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell232-34">[33]</a></sup>  An individual exposed to a rapid decompression (high rate of ascent) above 5,500 metres (18,000 ft) has a greater risk of altitude DCS than being exposed to the same altitude but at a lower rate of ascent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>the duration of exposure – the longer the duration of the dive, the greater is the risk of DCS. Longer flights, especially to altitudes of 5,500 m (18,000 ft) and above, carry a greater risk of altitude DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li>underwater diving before flying – divers who ascend to altitude soon after a dive increase their risk of developing DCS even if the dive itself was within the dive table safe limits. Dive tables make provisions for post-dive time at surface level before flying to allow any residual excess nitrogen to outgas. However, the pressure maintained inside even a pressurized aircraft may be as low as the pressure equivalent to an altitude of 2,400 m (7,900 ft) above sea level. Therefore, the assumption that the dive table surface interval occurs at normal atmospheric pressure is invalidated by flying during that surface interval, and an otherwise-safe dive may then exceed the dive table limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bassett_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAD_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAD-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17672177_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17672177-37">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>diving before travelling to altitude – DCS can occur without flying if the person moves to a high-altitude location on land immediately after diving, for example, scuba divers in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> who drive from the coast to the <a href="/wiki/Asmara" title="Asmara">Asmara</a> plateau at 2,400 m (7,900 ft) increase their risk of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell79_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell79-38">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Altitude_diving" title="Altitude diving">diving at altitude</a> – diving in water whose surface altitude is above 300 m (980 ft) — for example, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> is at 3,800 m (12,500 ft) — without using versions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_table" title="Decompression table">decompression tables</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dive_computer" title="Dive computer">dive computers</a> that are modified for high-altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bassett_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egi_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egi-39">[38]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual">Individual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Individual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atrial_septal_defect-en.png"><img alt="Diagram of the four chambers of the heart. There is a gap in the wall between the upper-left and upper-right chambers" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atrial_septal_defect-en.png/220px-Atrial_septal_defect-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atrial_septal_defect-en.png/330px-Atrial_septal_defect-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Atrial_septal_defect-en.png/440px-Atrial_septal_defect-en.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atrial_septal_defect-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atrial septal defect (PFO) showing left-to-right shunt. A right-to-left shunt may allow bubbles to pass into the arterial circulation.</div></div></div>
<p>The following individual factors have been identified as possibly contributing to increased risk of DCS:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> – Studies by Walder concluded that decompression sickness could be reduced in aviators when the serum surface tension was raised by drinking isotonic saline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walder_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walder-40">[39]</a></sup> and the high surface tension of water is generally regarded as helpful in controlling bubble size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup> Maintaining proper hydration is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell71_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell71-41">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patent_foramen_ovale" title="Patent foramen ovale">patent foramen ovale</a> – a hole between the atrial chambers of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> is normally closed by a flap with the first breaths at birth. In about 20% of adults the flap does not completely seal, however, allowing blood through the hole when coughing or during activities that raise chest pressure. In diving, this can allow venous blood with microbubbles of inert gas to bypass the lungs, where the bubbles would otherwise be filtered out by the lung capillary system, and return directly to the arterial system (including arteries to the brain, spinal cord and heart).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moon-42">[41]</a></sup> In the arterial system, bubbles (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_gas_embolism" title="Arterial gas embolism">arterial gas embolism</a>) are far more dangerous because they block circulation and cause <a href="/wiki/Infarction" title="Infarction">infarction</a> (tissue death, due to local loss of blood flow). In the brain, infarction results in <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, and in the spinal cord it may result in <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell70_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELippmann_&amp;_Mitchell70-43">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>a person's age – there are some reports indicating a higher risk of altitude DCS with increasing age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>previous injury – there is some indication that recent joint or limb injuries may predispose individuals to developing decompression-related bubbles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karlsson_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karlsson-44">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ambient" title="wikt:ambient">ambient</a> temperature – there is some evidence suggesting that individual exposure to very cold ambient temperatures may increase the risk of altitude DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup> Decompression sickness risk can be reduced by increased ambient temperature during decompression following dives in cold water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerth2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerth2-45">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>body type – typically, a person who has a high body fat content is at greater risk of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup> This is due to nitrogen's five times greater solubility in fat than in water, leading to greater amounts of total body dissolved nitrogen during time at pressure. Fat represents about 15–25 percent of a healthy adult's body, but stores about half of the total amount of nitrogen (about 1 litre) at normal pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fatness_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-fatness-46">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>alcohol consumption – although <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> consumption increases dehydration and therefore may increase susceptibility to DCS,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fryer_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Fryer-33">[32]</a></sup> a 2005 study found no evidence that alcohol consumption increases the incidence of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leigh2005_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-leigh2005-47">[46]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mechanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Preparing_for_recompression.jpg"><img alt="A diver being helped out of his bulky diving gear" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="839" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Preparing_for_recompression.jpg/170px-Preparing_for_recompression.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Preparing_for_recompression.jpg/255px-Preparing_for_recompression.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Preparing_for_recompression.jpg/340px-Preparing_for_recompression.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Preparing_for_recompression.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This surfacing diver must enter a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_chamber" title="Decompression chamber">decompression chamber</a> for <a href="/wiki/Decompression_practice#Surface_decompression" title="Decompression practice">surface decompression</a>, a standard operating procedure to avoid decompression sickness after long or deep <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_underwater_diving_terminology#bounce_dive" title="Glossary of underwater diving terminology">bounce dives</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Physiology_of_decompression" title="Physiology of decompression">Physiology of decompression</a></div>
<p>Depressurisation causes <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gases</a>, which were dissolved under higher <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, to come out of physical <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solution</a> and form gas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_bubble" title="Liquid bubble">bubbles</a> within the body. These bubbles produce the symptoms of decompression sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38uhms_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-38uhms-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ackles_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ackles-48">[47]</a></sup> Bubbles may form whenever the body experiences a reduction in pressure, but not all bubbles result in DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishi_Brubakk_&amp;_Eftedal501_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishi_Brubakk_&amp;_Eftedal501-49">[48]</a></sup>  The amount of gas dissolved in a liquid is described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry%27s_Law" title="Henry's Law">Henry's Law</a>, which indicates that when the pressure of a gas in contact with a liquid is decreased, the amount of that gas dissolved in the liquid will also decrease proportionately.
</p><p>On ascent from a dive, inert gas comes out of solution in a process called "<a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a>" or "offgassing". Under normal conditions, most offgassing occurs by <a href="/wiki/Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange">gas exchange</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kindwall1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kindwall1-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kindwall2_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kindwall2-51">[50]</a></sup> If inert gas comes out of solution too quickly to allow outgassing in the lungs then bubbles may form in the blood or within the solid tissues of the body. The formation of bubbles in the skin or joints results in milder symptoms, while large numbers of bubbles in the venous blood can cause lung damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''583–584_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Manifestations''583–584-52">[51]</a></sup> The most severe types of DCS interrupt — and ultimately damage — spinal cord function, leading to <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_system" title="Sensory system">sensory</a> dysfunction, or death. In the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Right-to-left_shunt" title="Right-to-left shunt">right-to-left shunt</a> of the heart, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patent_foramen_ovale" title="Patent foramen ovale">patent foramen ovale</a>, venous bubbles may enter the arterial system, resulting in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_gas_embolism" title="Arterial gas embolism">arterial gas embolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Pathophysiology''530–541_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrancis_&amp;_Mitchell,_''Pathophysiology''530–541-53">[52]</a></sup> A similar effect, known as <a href="/wiki/Ebullism" title="Ebullism">ebullism</a>, may occur during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explosive_decompression" title="Explosive decompression">explosive decompression</a>, when water vapour forms bubbles in body fluids due to a dramatic reduction in environmental pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landis_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landis-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inert_gases">Inert gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Inert gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main inert gas in air is <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, but nitrogen is not the only gas that can cause DCS. <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">Breathing gas</a> mixtures such as <a href="/wiki/Trimix_(breathing_gas)" title="Trimix (breathing gas)">trimix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heliox" title="Heliox">heliox</a> include <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, which can also cause decompression sickness. Helium both enters and leaves the body faster than nitrogen, so different decompression schedules are required, but, since helium does not cause <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_narcosis" title="Nitrogen narcosis">narcosis</a>, it is preferred over nitrogen in gas mixtures for deep diving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann475_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann475-55">[54]</a></sup>
There is some debate as to the decompression requirements for helium during short-duration dives. Most divers do longer decompressions; however, some groups like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WKPP" title="WKPP">WKPP</a> have been pioneering the use of shorter decompression times by including <a href="/wiki/Decompression_practice#Deep_stops" title="Decompression practice">deep stops</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wienke2002_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wienke2002-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Any inert gas that is breathed under pressure can form bubbles when the ambient pressure decreases. Very deep dives have been made using <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>-oxygen mixtures (<a href="/wiki/Hydrox_(breathing_gas)" title="Hydrox (breathing gas)">hydrox</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fife_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-fife-57">[56]</a></sup> but controlled decompression is still required to avoid DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uhms33_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-uhms33-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isobaric_counterdiffusion">Isobaric counterdiffusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Isobaric counterdiffusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Isobaric_counterdiffusion" title="Isobaric counterdiffusion">Isobaric counterdiffusion</a></div>
<p>DCS can also be caused at a constant ambient pressure when switching between gas mixtures containing different proportions of inert gas. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Isobaric_counterdiffusion" title="Isobaric counterdiffusion">isobaric counterdiffusion</a>, and presents a problem for very deep dives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann477_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann477-59">[58]</a></sup> For example, after using a very helium-rich <a href="/wiki/Trimix_(breathing_gas)" title="Trimix (breathing gas)">trimix</a> at the deepest part of the dive, a diver will switch to mixtures containing progressively less helium and more oxygen and nitrogen during the ascent. Nitrogen diffuses into tissues 2.65 times slower than helium, but is about 4.5 times more soluble. Switching between gas mixtures that have very different fractions of nitrogen and helium can result in "fast" tissues (those tissues that have a good blood supply) actually increasing their total inert gas loading. This is often found to provoke inner ear decompression sickness, as the ear seems particularly sensitive to this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burton2004_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-burton2004-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bubble_formation">Bubble formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bubble formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The location of micronuclei or where bubbles initially form is not known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papadopoulou_2013_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Papadopoulou_2013-61">[60]</a></sup> The most likely mechanisms for bubble formation are <a href="/wiki/Tribonucleation" title="Tribonucleation">tribonucleation</a>, when two surfaces make and break contact (such as in joints), and heterogeneous <a href="/wiki/Nucleation" title="Nucleation">nucleation</a>, where bubbles are created at a site based on a surface in contact with the liquid. Homogeneous nucleation, where bubbles form within the liquid itself is less likely because it requires much greater pressure differences than experienced in decompression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papadopoulou_2013_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Papadopoulou_2013-61">[60]</a></sup> The spontaneous formation of nanobubbles on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> surfaces is a possible source of micronuclei, but it is not yet clear if these can grow large enough to cause symptoms as they are very stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papadopoulou_2013_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Papadopoulou_2013-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once microbubbles have formed, they can grow by either a reduction in pressure or by diffusion of gas into the gas from its surroundings. In the body, bubbles may be located within tissues or carried along with the bloodstream. The speed of blood flow within a blood vessel and the rate of delivery of blood to capillaries (<a href="/wiki/Perfusion" title="Perfusion">perfusion</a>) are the main factors that determine whether dissolved gas is taken up by tissue bubbles or circulation bubbles for bubble growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Papadopoulou_2013_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Papadopoulou_2013-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary provoking agent in decompression sickness is bubble formation from excess dissolved gases. Various hypotheses have been put forward for the nucleation and growth of bubbles in tissues, and for the level of supersaturation which will support bubble growth. The earliest bubble formation detected is subclinical intravascular bubbles detectable by doppler ultrasound in the venous systemic circulation. The presence of these "silent" bubbles is no guarantee that they will persist and grow to be symptomatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vascular bubbles formed in the systemic capillaries may be trapped in the lung  capillaries, temporarily blocking them. If this is severe, the symptom called "chokes" may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann_1989_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vann_1989-63">[62]</a></sup> If the diver has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patent_foramen_ovale" title="Patent foramen ovale">patent foramen ovale</a> (or a <a href="/wiki/Shunt_(medical)" title="Shunt (medical)">shunt</a> in the pulmonary circulation), bubbles may pass through it and bypass the pulmonary circulation to enter the arterial blood. If these bubbles are not absorbed in the arterial plasma and lodge in systemic capillaries they will block the flow of oxygenated blood to the tissues supplied by those capillaries, and those tissues will be starved of oxygen. Moon and Kisslo (1988) concluded that "the evidence suggests that the risk of serious neurological DCI or early onset DCI is increased in divers with a resting right-to-left shunt through a PFO. There is, at present, no evidence that PFO is related to mild or late onset bends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moon1998_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moon1998-64">[63]</a></sup>
Bubbles form within other tissues as well as the blood vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann_1989_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vann_1989-63">[62]</a></sup> Inert gas can diffuse into bubble nuclei between tissues. In this case, the bubbles can distort and permanently damage the tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spira_1999_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spira_1999-65">[64]</a></sup> As they grow, the bubbles may also compress nerves, causing pain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephenson_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephenson-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Medscape_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Medscape-67">[66]</a></sup><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extravascular" title="Extravascular">Extravascular</a> or autochthonous<sup class="reference" id="ref_a"><a href="#endnote_a">[a]</a></sup> bubbles usually form in slow tissues such as joints, tendons and muscle sheaths. Direct expansion causes tissue damage, with the release of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histamines" title="Histamines">histamines</a> and their associated affects. Biochemical damage may be as important as, or more important than mechanical effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephenson_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stephenson-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vann_1989_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vann_1989-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitano_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitano-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bubble size and growth may be affected by several factors - gas exchange with adjacent tissues, the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfactants" title="Surfactants">surfactants</a>, coalescence and disintegration by collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245-62">[61]</a></sup> Vascular bubbles may cause direct blockage, aggregate platelets and red blood cells, and trigger the coagulation process, causing local and downstream clotting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spira_1999_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spira_1999-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arteries may be blocked by intravascular fat aggregation. <a href="/wiki/Platelet" title="Platelet">Platelets</a> accumulate in the vicinity of bubbles. <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">Endothelial</a> damage may be a mechanical effect of bubble pressure on the vessel walls, a toxic effect of stabilised platelet aggregates and possibly toxic effects due to the association of lipids with the air bubbles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245-62">[61]</a></sup> Protein molecules may be denatured by reorientation of the secondary and tertiary structure when non-polar groups protrude into the bubble gas and hydrophilic groups remain in the surrounding blood, which may generate a cascade of pathophysiological events with consequent production of clinical signs of decompression sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986241-245-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>The physiological effects of a reduction in environmental pressure depend on the rate of bubble growth, the site, and surface activity. A sudden release of sufficient pressure in saturated tissue results in a complete disruption of cellular organelles, while a more gradual reduction in pressure may allow accumulation of a smaller number of larger bubbles, some of which may not produce clinical signs, but still cause physiological effects typical of a blood/gas interface and mechanical effects. Gas is dissolved in all tissues, but decompression sickness is only clinically recognised in the central nervous system, bone, ears, teeth, skin and lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Necrosis has frequently been reported in the lower cervical, thoracic, and upper lumbar regions of the spinal cord. A catastrophic pressure reduction from saturation produces explosive mechanical disruption of cells by local effervescence, while a more gradual pressure loss tends to produce discrete bubbles accumulated in the white matter, surrounded by a protein layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254-69">[68]</a></sup> Typical acute spinal decompression injury occurs in the columns of white matter. Infarcts are characterised by a region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oedema" title="Oedema">oedema</a>, haemorrhage and early <a href="/wiki/Myelin" title="Myelin">myelin</a> degeneration, and are typically centred on small blood vessels. The lesions are generally discrete. Oedema usually extends to the adjacent grey matter. <a href="/wiki/Thrombus" title="Thrombus">Microthrombi</a> are found in the blood vessels associated with the infarcts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the acute changes there is an invasion of lipid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phagocytes" title="Phagocytes">phagocytes</a> and degeneration of adjacent neural fibres with vascular <a href="/wiki/Hyperplasia" title="Hyperplasia">hyperplasia</a> at the edges of the infarcts. The lipid phagocytes are later replaced by a cellular reaction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astrocytes" title="Astrocytes">astrocytes</a>. Vessels in surrounding areas remain patent but are <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagenised</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254-69">[68]</a></sup> Distribution of spinal cord lesions may be related to vascular supply. There is still uncertainty regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aetiology" title="Aetiology">aetiology</a> of decompression sickness damage to the spinal cord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986246-254-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dysbaric_osteonecrosis" title="Dysbaric osteonecrosis">Dysbaric osteonecrosis</a> lesions are typically bilateral and usually occur at both ends of the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> and at the proximal end of the <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> Symptoms are usually only present when a joint surface is involved, which typically does not occur until a long time after the causative exposure to a hyperbaric environment. The initial damage is attributed to the formation of bubbles, and one episode can be sufficient, however incidence is sporadic and generally associated with relatively long periods of hyperbaric exposure and aetiology is uncertain. Early identification of lesions by <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> is not possible, but over time areas of radiographic opacity develop in association with the damaged bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalder_1986254-258_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalder_1986254-258-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Decompression sickness should be suspected if any of the symptoms associated with the condition occurs following a drop in pressure, in particular, within 24 hours of diving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThalmannDAN_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ThalmannDAN-71">[70]</a></sup> In 1995, 95% of all cases reported to <a href="/wiki/Divers_Alert_Network" title="Divers Alert Network">Divers Alert Network</a> had shown symptoms within 24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAN1995annual_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAN1995annual-72">[71]</a></sup> An alternative diagnosis should be suspected if severe symptoms begin more than six hours following decompression without an altitude exposure or if any symptom occurs more than 24 hours after surfacing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoonSPUMS98_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-MoonSPUMS98-73">[72]</a></sup> The diagnosis is confirmed if the symptoms are relieved by recompression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MoonSPUMS98_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-MoonSPUMS98-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45UHMS_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-45UHMS-74">[73]</a></sup> Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">MRI</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">CT</a> can frequently identify bubbles in DCS, they are not as good at determining the diagnosis as a proper history of the event and description of the symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1908_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1908-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underwater_diving">Underwater diving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Underwater diving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aladin-pdc.jpg"><img alt="Close-up of the LCD display of an Aladin Pro" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="856" data-file-width="756" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aladin-pdc.jpg/220px-Aladin-pdc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aladin-pdc.jpg/330px-Aladin-pdc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Aladin-pdc.jpg/440px-Aladin-pdc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aladin-pdc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The display of a basic personal dive computer shows depth, dive time, and decompression information.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Decompression_practice" title="Decompression practice">Decompression practice</a></div>
<p>To prevent the excess formation of bubbles that can lead to decompression sickness, divers limit their ascent rate—the recommended ascent rate used by popular decompression models is about 10 metres (33 ft) per minute—and carry out a decompression schedule as necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471-75">[74]</a></sup> This schedule requires the diver to ascend to a particular depth, and remain at that depth until sufficient gas has been eliminated from the body to allow further ascent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann455_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann455-76">[75]</a></sup> Each of these is termed a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_stop" title="Decompression stop">decompression stop</a>", and a schedule for a given bottom time and depth may contain one or more stops, or none at all. Dives that contain no decompression stops are called "no-stop dives", but divers usually schedule a short "safety stop" at 3 m (10 ft), 4.6 m (15 ft), or 6 m (20 ft), depending on the training agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471-75">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stops_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-stops-77">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>The decompression schedule may be derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_tables" title="Decompression tables">decompression tables</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dive_tables#Alternatives" title="Dive tables">decompression software</a>, or from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dive_computers" title="Dive computers">dive computers</a>, and these are commonly based upon a mathematical model of the body's uptake and release of inert gas as pressure changes. These models, such as the Bühlmann decompression algorithm, are designed to fit empirical data and provide a decompression schedule for a given depth and dive duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann456–457_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann456–457-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since divers on the surface after a dive still have excess inert gas in their bodies, decompression from any subsequent dive before this excess is fully eliminated needs to modify the schedule to take account of the residual gas load from the previous dive. This will result in a shorter allowable time under water without obligatory decompression stops, or an increased decompression time during the subsequent dive. The total elimination of excess gas may take many hours, and tables will indicate the time at normal pressures that is required, which may be up to 18 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471–473_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann471–473-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Decompression time can be significantly shortened by breathing mixtures containing much less inert gas during the decompression phase of the dive (or pure <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> at stops in 6 metres (20 ft) of water or less). The reason is that the inert gas outgases at a rate proportional to the difference between the <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> of inert gas in the diver's body and its partial pressure in the breathing gas; whereas the likelihood of bubble formation depends on the difference between the inert gas partial pressure in the diver's body and the ambient pressure. Reduction in decompression requirements can also be gained by breathing a <a href="/wiki/Nitrox" title="Nitrox">nitrox</a> mix during the dive, since less nitrogen will be taken into the body than during the same dive done on air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann474–475_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann474–475-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following a decompression schedule does not completely protect against DCS. The algorithms used are designed to reduce the probability of DCS to a very low level, but do not reduce it to zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann456_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton_&amp;_Thalmann456-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure_to_altitude">Exposure to altitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Exposure to altitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the most significant breakthroughs in the prevention of altitude DCS is oxygen pre-breathing.  Breathing pure oxygen significantly reduces the nitrogen loads in body tissues by reducing the partial pressure of nitrogen in the lungs, which induces diffusion of nitrogen from the blood into the breathing gas, and this effect eventually lowers the concentration of nitrogen in the other tissues of the body. If continued for long enough, and without interruption, this provides effective protection upon exposure to low-barometric pressure environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis-22">[21]</a></sup> However, breathing pure oxygen during flight alone (ascent, en route, descent) does not decrease the risk of altitude DCS as the time required for ascent is generally not sufficient to significantly desaturate the slower tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dehart_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-Dehart-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilmanis_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pilmanis-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure aviator oxygen which has moisture removed to prevent freezing of valves at altitude is readily available and routinely used in general aviation mountain flying and at high altitudes.  Most small general aviation aircraft are not pressurized, therefore oxygen use is an FAA requirement at higher altitudes.
</p><p>Although pure oxygen pre-breathing is an effective method to protect against altitude DCS, it is logistically complicated and expensive for the protection of civil aviation flyers, either commercial or private. Therefore, it is currently used only by military flight crews and astronauts for protection during high-altitude and space operations. It is also used by flight test crews involved with certifying aircraft, and may also be used for high-altitude parachute jumps.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronauts" title="Astronauts">Astronauts</a> aboard the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> preparing for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">extra-vehicular activity</a> (EVA) "camp out" at low atmospheric pressure, 10.2 psi (0.70 bar), spending eight sleeping hours in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quest_airlock" title="Quest airlock">Quest airlock</a> chamber before their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacewalk" title="Spacewalk">spacewalk</a>. During the EVA they breathe 100% oxygen in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacesuit" title="Spacesuit">spacesuits</a>, which operate at 4.3 psi (0.30 bar),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tanner_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-tanner-82">[80]</a></sup> although research has examined the possibility of using 100% O<sub>2</sub> at 9.5 psi (0.66 bar) in the suits to lessen the pressure reduction, and hence the risk of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webb1989_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-webb1989-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg"><img alt="A large horizontal cylinder with a bank of instruments and monitors" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg/220px-Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg/330px-Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg/440px-Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_decompression_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The recompression chamber at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutral_Buoyancy_Lab" title="Neutral Buoyancy Lab">Neutral Buoyancy Lab</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_medicine" title="Hyperbaric medicine">Hyperbaric medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_treatment_schedules" title="Hyperbaric treatment schedules">Hyperbaric treatment schedules</a></div>
<p>All cases of decompression sickness should be treated initially with 100% oxygen until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_oxygen_therapy" title="Hyperbaric oxygen therapy">hyperbaric oxygen therapy</a> (100% oxygen delivered in a high-pressure chamber) can be provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1912_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1912-84">[82]</a></sup> Mild cases of the "bends" and some skin symptoms may disappear during descent from high altitude; however, it is recommended that these cases still be evaluated. Neurological symptoms, pulmonary symptoms, and mottled or marbled skin lesions should be treated with hyperbaric oxygen therapy if seen within 10 to 14 days of development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1813_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1813-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recompression on air was shown to be an effective treatment for minor DCS symptoms by Keays in 1909.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keays1909_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keays1909-86">[84]</a></sup> Evidence of the effectiveness of recompression therapy utilizing oxygen was first shown by Yarbrough and <a href="/wiki/Albert_R._Behnke" title="Albert R. Behnke">Behnke</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yarbrough1939_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yarbrough1939-87">[85]</a></sup> and has since become the standard of care for treatment of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berghage1978_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berghage1978-88">[86]</a></sup> Recompression is normally carried out in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recompression_chamber" title="Recompression chamber">recompression chamber</a>. At a dive site, a riskier alternative is <a href="/wiki/In-water_recompression" title="In-water recompression">in-water recompression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmonds1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds1998-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pyle1995_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pyle1995-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay108_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kay108-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_first_aid" title="Oxygen first aid">Oxygen first aid</a> has been used as an emergency treatment for diving injuries for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoon_&amp;_Gorman616_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoon_&amp;_Gorman616-92">[90]</a></sup> If given within the first four hours of surfacing, it increases the success of recompression therapy as well as decreasing the number of recompression treatments required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longphre_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longphre-93">[91]</a></sup> Most fully closed-circuit <a href="/wiki/Rebreather" title="Rebreather">rebreathers</a> can deliver sustained high concentrations of oxygen-rich <a href="/wiki/Breathing_gas" title="Breathing gas">breathing gas</a> and could be used as a means of supplying oxygen if dedicated equipment is not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goble2003_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goble2003-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is beneficial to give fluids, as this helps reduce <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a>. It is no longer recommended to administer aspirin, unless advised to do so by medical personnel, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analgesics" title="Analgesics">analgesics</a> may mask symptoms. People should be made comfortable and placed in the supine position (horizontal), or the <a href="/wiki/Recovery_position" title="Recovery position">recovery position</a> if vomiting occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThalmannDAN_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-ThalmannDAN-71">[70]</a></sup> In the past, both the <a href="/wiki/Trendelenburg_position" title="Trendelenburg position">Trendelenburg position</a> and the left lateral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decubitus_position" title="Decubitus position">decubitus position</a> (Durant's maneuver) have been suggested as beneficial where air emboli are suspected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Dowd2000_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Dowd2000-95">[93]</a></sup> but are no longer recommended for extended periods, owing to concerns regarding <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_edema" title="Cerebral edema">cerebral edema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoon_&amp;_Gorman616_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoon_&amp;_Gorman616-92">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bove2009_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bove2009-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The duration of recompression treatment depends on the severity of symptoms, the dive history, the type of recompression therapy used and the patient's response to the treatment. One of the more frequently used <a href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_treatment_schedules" title="Hyperbaric treatment schedules">treatment schedules</a> is the US Navy Table 6, which provides hyperbaric oxygen therapy with a maximum pressure equivalent to 60 feet (18 m) of seawater for a total time under pressure of 288 minutes, of which 240 minutes are on oxygen and the balance are air breaks to minimise the possibility of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">oxygen toxicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNDM_Table_6_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNDM_Table_6-97">[95]</a></sup>
