</p><p>A multiplace chamber is the preferred facility for treatment of decompression sickness as it allows direct physical access to the patient by medical personnel, but monoplace chambers are more widely available and should be used for treatment if a multiplace chamber is not available or transportation would cause significant delay in treatment, as the interval between onset of symptoms and recompression is important to the quality of recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kindwall_1988_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kindwall_1988-98">[96]</a></sup> It may be necessary to modify the optimum treatment schedule  to allow use of a monoplace chamber, but this is usually better than delaying treatment. A US Navy treatment table 5 can be safely performed without air breaks if a built-in breathing system is not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kindwall_1988_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kindwall_1988-98">[96]</a></sup> In most cases the patient can be adequately treated in a monoplace chamber at the receiving hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kindwall_1988_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kindwall_1988-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Immediate treatment with 100% oxygen, followed by recompression in a hyperbaric chamber, will in most cases result in no long-term effects. However, permanent long-term injury from DCS is possible. Three-month follow-ups on diving accidents reported to DAN in 1987 showed 14.3% of the 268 divers surveyed had ongoing symptoms of Type II DCS, and 7% from Type I DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UHMS43_Bennett_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-UHMS43_Bennett-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAN88_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAN88-100">[98]</a></sup> Long-term follow-ups showed similar results, with 16% having permanent neurological sequelae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desola1989_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desola1989-101">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The incidence of decompression sickness is rare, estimated at 2.8 cases per 10,000 dives, with the risk 2.6 times greater for males than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1908_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1908-3">[3]</a></sup> DCS affects approximately 1,000 U.S. scuba divers per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ThalmannDAN_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-ThalmannDAN-71">[70]</a></sup> In 1999, the <a href="/wiki/Divers_Alert_Network" title="Divers Alert Network">Divers Alert Network</a> (DAN) created "Project Dive Exploration" to collect data on dive profiles and incidents. From 1998 to 2002, they recorded 50,150 dives, from which 28 recompressions were required — although these will almost certainly contain incidents of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_gas_embolism" title="Arterial gas embolism">arterial gas embolism</a> (AGE) — a rate of about 0.05%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-emed-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pde_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-pde-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 2013, Honduras had the highest number of decompression-related deaths and disabilities in the world, caused by unsafe practices in <a href="/wiki/Miskito_people#The_lobster_economy_and_controversy" title="Miskito people">lobster diving among the indigenous Miskito people</a>, who face great economic pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miskito_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miskito-103">[101]</a></sup> At that time it was estimated that in the country over 2000 divers had been injured and 300 others had died since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miskito_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miskito-103">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>1670: <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> demonstrated that a reduction in ambient pressure could lead to bubble formation in living tissue. This description of a bubble forming in the eye of a <a href="/wiki/Viperidae" title="Viperidae">viper</a> subjected to a near vacuum was the first recorded description of decompression sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-law_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-law-104">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>1769: <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Morgagni" title="Giovanni Battista Morgagni">Giovanni Morgagni</a> described the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_mortem" title="Post mortem">post mortem</a> findings of air in <a href="/wiki/Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum">cerebral</a> <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulation</a> and surmised that this was the cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1840: <a href="/wiki/Charles_Pasley" title="Charles Pasley">Charles Pasley</a>, who was involved in the recovery of the sunken warship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Royal_George_(1756)" title="HMS Royal George (1756)">HMS <i>Royal George</i></a>, commented that, of those having made frequent dives, "not a man escaped the repeated attacks of <a href="/wiki/Rheumatism" title="Rheumatism">rheumatism</a> and cold".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarx1903_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarx1903-106">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li>1841: First documented case of decompression sickness, reported by a mining engineer who observed pain and muscle cramps among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_miner" title="Coal miner">coal miners</a> working in <a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">mine shafts</a> air-pressurized to keep water out.</li>
<li>1854: Decompression sickness reported and one resulting death of <a href="/wiki/Caisson_(engineering)" title="Caisson (engineering)">caisson</a> workers on the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Bridge" title="Royal Albert Bridge">Royal Albert Bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-albert_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-albert-107">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li>1870: Bauer published outcomes of 25 paralyzed caisson workers.
<dl><dd>From 1870 to 1910, all prominent features were established. Explanations at the time included: cold or exhaustion causing reflex spinal cord damage; electricity cause by <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> on compression; or organ <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/congestion" title="wikt:congestion">congestion</a>; and vascular stasis caused by decompression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg"><img alt="A large arch bridge with several substantial support pillars sunk into a river" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1289" data-file-width="5237" decoding="async" height="54" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg/220px-Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg/330px-Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg/440px-Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eads_Bridge_panorama_20090119.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Eads Bridge where 42 workers were injured by caisson disease</div></div></div></dd></dl></li>
<li>1871: The <a href="/wiki/Eads_Bridge" title="Eads Bridge">Eads Bridge</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Louis" title="St Louis">St Louis</a> employed 352 compressed air workers including <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alphonse_Jaminet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alphonse Jaminet (page does not exist)">Alphonse Jaminet</a> as the physician in charge. There were 30 seriously injured and 12 fatalities. Jaminet developed decompression sickness and his personal description was the first such recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eads_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eads-24">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li>1872: The similarity between decompression sickness and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iatrogenic" title="Iatrogenic">iatrogenic</a> air embolism as well as the relationship between inadequate decompression and decompression sickness was noted by Friedburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-2"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup> He suggested that intravascular gas was released by rapid decompression and recommended: slow compression and decompression; four-hour working shifts; limit to maximum pressure of 44.1 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psig</a> (4 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>); using only healthy workers; and recompression treatment for severe cases.</li>
<li>1873: Andrew Smith first utilized the term "caisson disease" describing 110 cases of decompression sickness as the physician in charge during construction of the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">Brooklyn Bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eads_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eads-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooklyn_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brooklyn-108">[106]</a></sup> The project employed 600 compressed air workers. Recompression treatment was not used. The project chief engineer <a href="/wiki/Washington_Roebling" title="Washington Roebling">Washington Roebling</a> suffered from caisson disease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eads_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eads-24">[23]</a></sup> and endured the after-effects of the disease for the rest of his life. During this project, decompression sickness became known as "The Grecian Bends" or simply "the bends" because afflicted individuals characteristically bent forward at the hips: this is possibly reminiscent of a then popular women's fashion and dance maneuver known as the <a href="/wiki/Grecian_bend" title="Grecian bend">Grecian Bend</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eads_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eads-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCullogh_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCullogh-109">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>1890 During construction of the <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Hudson_Tubes" title="Downtown Hudson Tubes">Hudson River Tunnel</a> contractor's agent <a href="/wiki/Ernest_William_Moir" title="Ernest William Moir">Ernest William Moir</a> pioneered the use of an <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a> chamber for treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudson_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hudson-25">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>1900: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonard_Erskine_Hill" title="Leonard Erskine Hill">Leonard Hill</a> used a frog model to prove that decompression causes bubbles and that recompression resolves them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-3"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill1912_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill1912-110">[108]</a></sup> Hill advocated linear or uniform <a href="/wiki/Dive_profile" title="Dive profile">decompression profiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-4"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill1912_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hill1912-110">[108]</a></sup> This type of decompression is used today by <a href="/wiki/Saturation_diving" title="Saturation diving">saturation divers</a>. His work was financed by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Siebe" title="Augustus Siebe">Augustus Siebe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siebe_Gorman" title="Siebe Gorman">Siebe Gorman Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-5"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1904: Tunnel building to and from Manhattan Island caused over 3,000 injuries and over 30 deaths which led to laws requiring PSI limits and decompression rules for "sandhogs" in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1998_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1998-111">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>1904: Seibe and Gorman in conjunction with Leonard Hill developed and produced a closed bell in which a diver can be decompressed at the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daily_News_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daily_News-112">[110]</a></sup> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome,_Western_Australia.jpg"><img alt="A horizontal cylinder, just large enough to contain one person, with an hinged door clamp at one end" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1945" data-file-width="2940" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg/220px-Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg/330px-Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg/440px-Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome%2C_Western_Australia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_diving_recompression_chamber_at_Broome,_Western_Australia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early recompression chamber (door removed for public safety)</div></div></div></li>
<li>1908: "The Prevention of Compressed Air Illness" was published by <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Haldane" title="John Scott Haldane">JS Haldane</a>, Boycott and Damant recommending <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompression_stop" title="Decompression stop">staged decompression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boycott_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boycott-113">[111]</a></sup> These tables were accepted for use by the Royal Navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-6"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1914–16: Experimental decompression chambers were in use on land and aboard ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC_news_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC_news-114">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_1931_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_1931-115">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scott_1932_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_1932-116">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li>1924: The US Navy published the first standardized recompression procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNrecompression_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNrecompression-117">[115]</a></sup></li>
<li>1930s: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_R_Behnke" title="Albert R Behnke">Albert R Behnke</a> separated the symptoms of Arterial Gas Embolism (AGE) from those of DCS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-7"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1935: Behnke <i>et al.</i> experimented with oxygen for recompression therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-8"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNrecompression_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-USNrecompression-117">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behnke1935_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behnke1935-118">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>1937: Behnke introduced the "no-stop" decompression tables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acott1999-hist_105-9"><a href="#cite_note-acott1999-hist-105">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>1941: Altitude DCS is treated with hyperbaric oxygen for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid889546_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid889546-119">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li>1957: Robert Workman established a new method for calculation of decompression requirements (M-values).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Workman57_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Workman57-120">[118]</a></sup></li>
<li>1959: The "SOS Decompression Meter", a submersible mechanical device that simulated nitrogen uptake and release, was introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carson-121">[119]</a></sup></li>
<li>1960: FC Golding <i>et al.</i> split the classification of DCS into Type 1 and 2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid13850667_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid13850667-122">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li>1982: Paul K Weathersby, Louis D Homer and Edward T Flynn introduce <a href="/wiki/Survival_analysis" title="Survival analysis">survival analysis</a> into the study of decompression sickness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid6490468_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid6490468-123">[121]</a></sup></li>
<li>1983: Orca produced the "EDGE", a personal dive computer, using a <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessor</a> to calculate nitrogen absorption for twelve tissue compartments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carson_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carson-121">[119]</a></sup></li>
<li>1984: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_A_B%C3%BChlmann" title="Albert A Bühlmann">Albert A Bühlmann</a> released his book "Decompression–Decompression Sickness," which detailed his <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BChlmann_decompression_algorithm" title="Bühlmann decompression algorithm">deterministic model</a> for calculation of decompression schedules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buhlmann1984_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-buhlmann1984-124">[122]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, it is common for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_insurance" title="Medical insurance">medical insurance</a> not to cover treatment for the bends that is the result of recreational diving. This is because scuba diving is considered an elective and "high-risk" activity and treatment for decompression sickness is expensive. A typical stay in a recompression chamber will easily cost several thousand dollars, even before emergency transportation is included. As a result, groups such as <a href="/wiki/Divers_Alert_Network" title="Divers Alert Network">Divers Alert Network</a> (DAN) offer medical insurance policies that specifically cover all aspects of treatment for decompression sickness at rates of less than $100 per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DANinsurance_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-DANinsurance-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, treatment of DCS is provided by the National Health Service.  This may occur either at a specialised facility or at a hyperbaric centre based within a general hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LondonHyp_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-LondonHyp-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_2011_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson_2011-127">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Animals may also contract DCS, especially those caught in nets and rapidly brought to the surface.  It has been documented in loggerhead turtles and likely in prehistoric marine animals as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbitiss_2017_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbitiss_2017-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlsen_2017_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlsen_2017-129">[127]</a></sup> Modern reptiles are susceptible to DCS, and there is some evidence that marine mammals such as cetaceans and seals may also be affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piantadosi_and_Thalmann_2004_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piantadosi_and_Thalmann_2004-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sciencemag_2007_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sciencemag_2007-131">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becker_2015_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Becker_2015-132">[130]</a></sup> AW Carlsen has suggested that the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_shunt" title="Cardiac shunt">right-left shunt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Reptile#Circulation" title="Reptile">reptilian heart</a> may account for the predisposition in the same way as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patent_foramen_ovale" title="Patent foramen ovale">patent foramen ovale</a> does in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlsen_2017_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carlsen_2017-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ICD-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ICD_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> <a href="/wiki/Isobaric_counterdiffusion" title="Isobaric counterdiffusion">Inner ear counter diffusion</a> is a rare form of DCS sometimes experienced by divers engaged in <a href="/wiki/Deep_diving#Ultra-deep_diving" title="Deep diving">extreme deep diving</a>, caused by switching from a helium-rich gas to a nitrogen-rich gas at the start of a decompression stop. Although nitrogen diffuses more slowly than helium, nitrogen is much more soluble than helium and the total inert gas load in some tissues can temporarily exceed the critical supersaturation limit, resulting in bubble formation. The inner ear is particularly susceptible to this effect. Two of the best-recorded instances of it both occurred at <a href="/wiki/Boesmansgat" title="Boesmansgat">Boesmansgat</a>, South Africa — once to <a href="/wiki/Nuno_Gomes_(diver)" title="Nuno Gomes (diver)">Nuno Gomes</a> in an early world record attempt, and later to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Don_Shirley_(diver)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Don Shirley (diver) (page does not exist)">Don Shirley</a> when he tried to rescue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Shaw_(diver)" title="David Shaw (diver)">David Shaw</a> on his fateful dive trying to recover the body of <a href="/wiki/Deon_Dreyer" title="Deon Dreyer">Deon Dreyer</a>, who had been one of Gomes's support divers.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-stops-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-stops_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Tables based on US Navy tables, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAUI" title="NAUI">NAUI</a> tables have a safety stop at 15 feet (5 m);(<a href="#CITEREFLippmann_&amp;_Mitchell">Lippmann &amp; Mitchell</a>, p. 219) <a href="/wiki/British_Sub-Aqua_Club" title="British Sub-Aqua Club">BSAC</a> tables have a safety stop at 6 metres (20 ft); Bühlmann tables have a safety stop at 3 metres (10 ft).</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

medicine">Diving medicine portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Decompression_(diving)" title="Decompression (diving)"> Decompression (diving)</a> – The reduction of ambient pressure on underwater divers after hyperbaric exposure and the elimination of dissolved gases from the diver's tissues</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Decompression_illness" title="Decompression illness"> Decompression illness</a> – Disorders arising from ambient pressure reduction</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Decompression_theory" title="Decompression theory"> Decompression theory</a> – Theoretical modelling of decompression physiology</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taravana" title="Taravana"> Taravana</a> – Decompression sickness after breath-hold diving</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl><dd>1. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a">^a</a></b></span>  autochthonous: formed or originating in the place where found</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Decompression_sickness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-emed-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-emed_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-emed_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Pulley, Stephen A (27 November 2007). <a class="external text" href="http://emedicine.medscape.com/article/769717-overview" rel="nofollow">"Decompression Sickness"</a>.
